Dear Colleague,

The World Congress of Animal Welfare Societies held
in London in May created a great deal of interest and we
have had requests for copies of the speeches. If your
delegates read a paper, would you care to duplicate it
and send copies to the Societies which took part ? We are
enclosing their names and addresses herewith.

The Programme is enclosed, also a short account of
the proceedings. There were 54 speeches . Some 150 news
papers in England and on the Continent reported the pro~
ceedings very favourably.

Copies of the Resumes of Speeches are still available
If you would like to have them, please let us know whether
yeu want them in French, German or English.

We were very happy to meet the delegates and to hear
the work being done all over the World and we are looking
forward to meeting our colleagues again in four years'
time at the next World Congress.

Meanwhile we hope your Society will join with us
and many others all over the World in celebrating WORLD
Why FOR ANIMALS, on OCTOBER 4th.

YOurs sincerely,

Secretaryo,

 




WORLD CONGRESS

ANIMAL WELFARE SOCIETIES
MAY 1954

Press Ofﬁcer: KEIDRYCH RHYS
10 SEYMOUR ST., LONDON, W.1

Telephone WELbeck 5511 (8 lines)

The Congress was very successful. Delegates expressed
their satisfaction at having this opportunity to meet and listen to
their colleagues from other lands. All the Overseas visitors said
their journey was well worth while. 150 delegates attended from 117
Societies; 27 countries were represented.

The Congress opened with a Social Evening on Sunday,
Hay 16th, which was very well attended by delegates and Press
representatives . It was an opportunity for meeting and talking to
people which is not always possible during the Congress itself.Many
valuable contacts were made.

On Monday morning, May 17th, Lord Burden, the Chairman,
opened the proceedings with a short speech of welcome to the delegates.
This was followed by an exhibition of training of dogs by P.C.Crosswmy
and his intelligent companion and friend, Barry, an‘Alsatian. Then
followed speeches by various people according to the programme.

The afternoon opened with Dr Hugenholtz in the Chair and
closed at 5.30 p.m. The rest of the evening was given up to private
meetings of The World Federation for the Protection of Animals.

Tuesday was opened at 9.30 a.m. by Lord Burden and in the
afternoon, Mr Risdon took the Chair. At 5.30 coaches carried dele~
gates to St.Martin—in-the-Wields where an impressive service of
Intercession for the animals was held. The Bidding was written and
spoken by Major Lisle Watson, read in German by Dr Young and in

rench by Mrs Stott. Afterwards delegates returned to Caxton Hall
for a show of films arranged by UFAWS.

On Wednesday morning, Lord Burden took the Chair at
9.30 a.n. and in the afternoon wr Risden again officiated from 2.C
to 3.30. Then .70 Overseas del egates went by coach to Westminster
City Hall where they were received by the Mayor and Mayoress and
entertained to a delightful tea. Short speeches were made by the
Mayor and Dr Hugenholta, President of The World Federation.

At 4.50 there was an exhibition of the training of dogs,
specially for traffic, at the Caxton Hall, followed by a display of
humane education by means of Puppets given by nr Sharp.

’The Congress ended with a Dinner attended by 65 delegates
at which Lord Burden presided. This was an excellent vegetarian
meal provided by the Hotel Rubens. Short speeches were made by a
number of people and everyone seemed to enjoy the evening.

Before the Congress about 20 visi;tors walked through
the City of London in charge of a well~known London Guide, and
after the Congress, there were visits to the Houses of Parliament
with Major Lisle Watson as guide, and coach tours to London Clinic;
and shelters and to Worthiam to St.Francis Fields of Rest.

THE ANIMALS’ DEFENDER

July, I954

 

Alexis Carrell wrote in 1939: “ The spirit of Claude
Bernard still illuminates medicine. His methods and his
discoveries are extolled by scientists all over the world.”
After reading the foregoing there can be no doubt in any
one’s mind that this is shockingly true of Japan.

 

WORLD .CONGRESS IN LONDON

A Congress of animal welfare societies was held at Caxton
Hall, Westminster, on the three days May 17-19. It was
entitled to describe itself as a “ world ” one to this extent,
that about two—thirds of the 140 delegates were from
abroad and represented 27 different countries.

A captious critic might suggest that gatherings of this
kind produce nothing but an enormously long series of
“ papers ” and resolutions addressed entirely to the
already converted. 7

However, that is an unfair curtailment of the whole
picture. Most of the speakers at the Congress were
experts in some particular branch of animal welfare work.
Thus they were often mines of information and enlighten-
ment to those concentrating on other ﬁelds.

A long morning session was devoted to the anti-
Vivisection cause, and—it is gratifying to be able to add——
the iniquity of cruel animal experimentation was repeatedly
referred to and condemned at other sessions.

It is impossible in this note even to list the scores of
subjects which were expounded and discussed. Among
the high lights we recall a speech by Dr. Dobbie, of
Manchester, in which he urged the vital humane importance
of attaching an electrode to the dog’s head when electrocu-
tion is employed for lethalizing. (See our note in “News
of the Month.”)

We were also intrigued by a Spanish gentleman who
declared that bull-ﬁghts were losing their popularity in
his country in favour of football, and it was foreign tourists
and American sailors on leave who lined the arenas.

Apart from the actual sessions, the Congress afforded
many opportunities for home and foreign delegates getting
together and exchanging ideas and aspirations. A Congress
vegetarian dinner, some sight—seeing, and a service at
St. Martin-in-the-Fields were included in the programme.

So far as we could judge, the necessarily complicated
running of the Congress worked smoothly—a result due, we
lgelieve, mainly to its experienced secretary, Mrs. Y. M.

Iott.

A fair amount of press publicity was obtained, though we

should have been gladof more.

 

 

THE ELEPHANT’S KEEPER F ORGETS

One bright spot in the hateful picture of cruelty to
animals is that the newspapers now usually give wide
publicity to bad cases that get into court.

Some weeks ago a Ceylonese elephant “ boy ” (aged 56)
was ﬁned at Brighton for hitting a performing elephant on
the trunk with a block of wood. The animal’s alleged
offence was that of attempting to drink water which was
either too hot or too cold—the defendant, possibly owing
to imperfect command of the English language, did not

 

 

 

appear to know which. (The Bench was satisﬁed that
“ the animal was chastised harder than was necessary ”
but “ did not take a very serious view of the offence.”)

Among numerous press-cuttings on our desks is a very
full one from the Daily Telegraph, and we are bound to say
it makes most disturbing reading. An expert from the
London Zoo pointed out that the trunk is a particularly
sensitive organ. A veterinary surgeon gave his opinion
that the wounds were caused by “ direct blows of fantastic
force.”

The “ boy ” was ﬁned £5, and the R.S.P.C.A., which
brought the case, is to be congratulated on demonstrating
once more that it is not always safe in this country to ill-treat
animals.

Incidentally, if a ,6 5 ﬁne (there were also ,6 IO costs) is
considered adequate for what the magistrates regarded as a
“ not very serious ” crime, we may perhaps expect really
drastic punishment when the offence unquestionably is

- serious.

 

 

STRANGE BUT TRUE

The following strange and highly intriguing story
appeared in the South ’Wales Argus. It concerns a cat and a
mouse, only instead of the former catching and killing the
latter, it acted as a devoted foster-mother.

At a South Wales farm a stray female cat arrived and
settled down, as some cats will in the vicinity of farms.
Later the settler had kittens in a haystack. One of the furry
babes was left to console the mother. Imagine the astonish-
ment of the farmer’s wife when, on going to look how things
were progressing in the haystack home, she discovered that
pussy was suckling a mouse as well as her own lawful
offspring !

No one knows how or why this miniature preﬁguring of
the lion lying down with the lamb came about. Nor does
the Argus reporter record what happened eventually.

As every naturalist is aware, the parent-child relationship
dwindles in the sub-human creatures as soon as the progeny
are able to fend for themselves. This is obviously a necessity
of survival. (Incidentally, it is nice to know that animals
need never fear the psychologists’ horrid Oedipus complex

'or father—ﬁxation.) However, our own experience (of dogs)

is that as long as the family remain together, Mother
continues to rule the roost for years after she has ceased to
provide the provender.

So let us hope that this feline foster-parent continued to
be kind to her adopted Mickey Mouse so long as their
companionship lasted, and that Mickey did not go out into
the world without a clear understanding that no other cat
was like mother.

 

 

Spreading the Light—Mr. Stephen Jay, B.A., gave an anti-
vivisection talk to Eastern Avenue Methodist Youth Club on
the evening of May 20. The Society’s secretary, Mr. Geoffrey
Bowker, addressed the Ethical Society Junior Discussion Group
at Conway Hall, London, on June 11. The Methodist Club has
since enrolled as a joint Member of the Society.

with the encephalograph—a measuring instrument which
registers the activity of the brain during an electric shock-—
had shown that the animal might be in agony for up to
12 seconds after receiving the initial charge.

This opens up the possibility of dreadful suffering in the
past. Some humanitarians may blame the R.S.P.C.A.
for not having adopted long ago the suggested improvement
of applying the current to the animal’s head instead of to
its neck. Such strictures, however, would not be quite
fair. Neither the Council nor the ofﬁcials of the
R.S.P.C.A. can be expected to be experts on the highly
technical problems of electric killing. They could but
follow the majority advice of the veterinary profession, and
this certiﬁed that their method was satisfactory. We also
gather that the employment of the headpiece electrode
might be not without dangers to the human operator.

The best that can be said of a bad—or at the very least a
highly dubious—business is that it has been stopped by the
leading animal protection society. We devoutly hope that
any other organizations which use the present electric
cabinet will hasten to do likewise—and that their
subscribers will demand, insist, and see that it is done
forthwith.

 

 

THE TORTURE OF DOGS AND APES
By M. Beddow Bayly, M.R.C.S., L.R.C.P.

If one wishes to obtain an accurate picture of what
actually takes place in a vivisection laboratory in terms
of pain and suffering to the animals concerned one must
read the uninhibited accounts of experiments which have
been recorded in Japanese scientiﬁc journals. For here
will be found an entire absence of squeamishness, a total
disregard for the feelings of experimental victims. They
provide evidence that the most severe mutilations have
been carried out on dogs and apes without a vestige of
anaesthesia or narcotic to assuage the pain.

In this country it is very difﬁcult to trace reports of
experiments on dogs, although the latest Parliamentary
Return shows that during 1952 there were 2,830, of which
1,147 were conducted without anaesthetics. True, we
have been repeatedly assured that no certiﬁcate dispensing
with the use of anaesthesia has been granted for any
operative procedure more severe than the giving of a
hypodermic injection or the lancing of a vein. But while
the clause in the Act of 1876 still remains which permits
experiments to be undertaken without the use of anaes-
thetics in cases where the use of an anaesthetic would
frustrate the object of the experiment, one may justly
maintain that such experiments are contemplated and may
at any time be permitted and performed in British
laboratories. However horrible they might. be, they could
be legally defended as having been conducted within the
provisions of the Cruelty to Animals Act.

Bearing this in mind and also the fact that investigations
pursued in one country are frequently repeated by research
workers in others with a View to conﬁrming or refuting
their results, it is not unreasonable to suggest that the
experiments now to be described might also take place in
Great Britain.

 

THE ANIMALS’ DEFENDER II

If it be argued that an anaesthetic would not have
interfered with the results of the experiment and therefore
could not be dispensed with in this country, then we are
obviously forced to convict the Japanese experimenter of
inﬂicting the most diabolical suffering without the
slightest excuse.

This particular investigation, published in The j‘apanese
journal of Physiology, Vol. 3, No. 1 (November 1952
p. 46) was concerned with the study of epileptic seizures
in dogs brought about by electrical or chemical stimulation
of the cortex (grey matter) of the brain. In a preliminary
series of experiments the author of the article injected a
large number of irritants (some 200, we are told) into the
brains of dogs and apes by means of a small metal capillary
tube. Some of them produced tonic convulsions (contin-
uous contractions), some clonic convulsions (rapidly
successive contractions), while others caused alternating
contractions, as in locomotion. As a variation to this, the
effect of sticking the injection needle alone into all the
various areas of the grey and white matter was studied.

The main experiment consisted in removing the greater
part of the vault of the skull (trepanning) in dogs and apes
so as to expose a large surface of the underlying brain.
The latter could then be stimulated either by chemical
irritants or by electric currents. During the whole of
these procedures, we are categorically told (p. 49) “ NO
NARCOTICS WERE USED.” The resulting convul-
sions of various muscles were recorded by tracings. When
the greater part of the brain surface had been mapped out
into areas associated with the epileptiform type of convul—
sions, portions of grey matter were sliced away and tracts
of nerve ﬁbres severed with a View to tracing the path of
electrical and chemical impulses. Having eliminated parts

of the cerebrum and cerebellum at different levels,

stimulation was continued and the resulting local or
generalized seizures noted.

It is impossible to ascertain how many dogs and apes
were used in this investigation, and only in a few instances
are tables given which show the length of time during
which the animals were fastened upon the vivisection
board having various portions of the brain extirpated and
others stimulated by a faradic current giving 40 to 80
shocks per second. It appears that the periods varied
from a few minutes to an hour and a half. If one may
believe the experimenter’s own comments, the animals
reactions, though very strong while the skull was being
opened, gave little or no trouble once this was done and
the dura mater* severed so as to expose the brain.

In conclusion, it should be mentioned that this particular
Japanese physiologist had, from his own account, been
engaged in accumulating information on this and similar
problems since 193 5; also, it is to be noted that a similar
series of experiments, having as their object the tracing of
the pathway of nerve impulses concerned with the move-
ments of the muscles of mastication, are recorded in
The Keio journal of Medicine, Vol. I, No. 3 (November
1952, p. 175) by the same physiologist. In this investi-
gation, too, involving the removal of a large part of the
skull and the stimulation of the brain by chemical agents
and electric currents, no anaesthetics whatever were used.

* Tough membrane forming the outer envelope of the brain.

2nd August 1956
Dear Christine,

I have been wading through the various State and Provincial Acts,
and having got to the end feel that I must have made many mistakes and
missed a lot of points, so I am hOping to get a UFAW member who has
read law to go through them again.

I enclose a copy of a letter that I have just sent to Mrs. Bryan.
I don’ t know at all how strong and well~informed the Defenders of Fur—
bearers are, so in case they can give me no replies I am letting you
have the questions as well. Are you in touch with them?

A few of the Provinces and States did not send their Acts. I don't
know whether it would be possible for you to get them. The Provinces
which didn' t send are Quebec, Prince Edward Island, and Newfoundland.
The States which did not send are Delaware, Florida, Idaho (?), Kentucky,
Nevada, New Hampshire, Ohio, and Washington.

I am not quite sure why you sent me the District of Columbia, where
apparently there is no trapping. Is this entirely an urban area? I am
sorry to be so ignorant.

 

I am afraid I have not wrestled with Mexico or Puerto Rico.

You kindly sent me the ”Abstract of Fur Laws“, but this is, of
course, not written from the humanitarian point of View and does not
pick out the points which are of interest to me. It was dated 1954-55;
if there is a more recent edition, could you send it to me?

I am sending you a copy of the Koran extracts in unplish, French
and Anabic by surface mail - I can 't remember if I sent this before.
Also, did I tell you we do not recommend the Electrothanator for
rabbits? We found the rabbits greatly objected to the wetting of the
ears, and that it took a long time to stop the heart.

YDurs.

J-eww» M...»-

 




 




Mrs. Bryan,

Defender§zof Furbearers,

P FROM PABI

 

 

 

By ART BUCHwAtD

 

What’s

Those of us in the Interna-
tional ’0 Set who live high on the
.l1.og live low on the goose. To
be mole speciﬁc,
we live on the
goose’s liver,
which we who
speak fluent
French call. foie
eras. No one
knows who dis-
covered that the
liver was the best
part of the goose,
though we know
that the Romans. .
as far back as 52
B. C. partook of Buchwald
this delectable luxury duiing
those Lucullan feasts that set
the pattern for modern-day
living.

It was not/until the happy

1eign of Louis XV that goose;

livei came into its own in F1 ance
A No1 man named Clause (the1e
aie some who say it was a Clause
named Norman), chef to g‘the
Marquis ‘de ‘Contades, then mili-
tary gotvernor of Strasbourg,
slipped some laid and; veal
aiound his goose liveis and then
tiied to cover the whole fishing
up with a toasted biown ciust.
Then he completely lost his head
and started stufﬁng trufﬂes from
Peiigmd in the cente1. The
malquis was entertaining a girl
friend at the time named Pate,
and when Clause (or Norman)
served the dish‘ she was so de—
lighted the marquis ordered that

NOrman.’s (or Clause’s) creation-

be called pate de foie gras.
A A

 

 

It did not take long befOre
Strasbourg became‘the pate de
foie gras capital of the world.
From then on no goose wassafe
in Alsace-Lorraine, and livei
trouble seemed to be the main
complaint.

Thedemand for goose liver
reached such proportions that

the French had to import“ livers

from the Danube countries, but
this source was cut off after the
war and French geese had to
carry the burden alone.

It is to the French farmers’
credit "that a goose is treated
with utmost care before the
moment of truth. She is given
a limitless amountof corn cooked
in a delicious mash With warm
water. Honking is permitted.

There comes a time when a
goose just can’t eat but that is
the moment when the farmer
wants .her to eat more. And so
the farmer grabs the goose gently
by the neck and sticks a funnel
in her mouth and grinds a kilo of
corn down her throat. This proc-
ess is done about three times a
day. Peasants will tell you the
geese look forward to this forced
feeding, and every time they see
a funnel the fowl rush up with
01-911 mouths.

Under good conditions the
goose’s lived is inﬂated far be-
yond her wildest dreams. When
the time comes, as it must to all
geese, the peasant gives the coup
de grace. The goose, who by this
tune suspects that this funnel

Good for the Goose

feeding is not inst for langhs,
shows no su1p1ise, and is so fed
up that she goes gratefully to the
block.

At this point, all the featheis
a1e plucked and the rest of the
goose is di essed f01 maiket. The
feathers ale used for down in
pillows and it’s very possible that
while one person is eating ,a
goose’ 5 liver anothe1 is sleeping
soundly on hei plumes. Chacuii
a son gout. .

_\ >1: *._ all

The liver is worked. over for
about. ten minutes and then
cooked by a secret recipe handed
down from generation to genera-
tion of. Strasbourg foie gras
maker.s

Then it is placed in teiiines
and sold for outiageous piices
th10ughout the world

The reason foie was is so ex--
pensive is that there is so much
demand in France for goose liv-
ers and so little demand for
goose. Alsatians are not goose
eaters and neither are the rest
of the French. The Germans are,
but as with everything else, the
Germans are raising their own
geese and the market for dressed
liverless goose is not very good.
So what people are really paying
for when they eat foie gras is not
the liver but the bird. ‘

The only place wher e the whole
goose is Welc0me is in Newfound-
land, where they have an old
saying; “What’s good for the;
goose is good for Gander.’

(President: Professor EDWARD HINDLE, M.A., Sc.D., Ph.D. F.R.S.

50th October l952
Mrs. Stevens,

The Animal Welfare Insitute,
730 Eifth Avenue,
New iork 19.

Dear Mrs. Stevens,

Hume thought you would like to see this cons of Hansard, as it
contains a debate on experiments on animals. I think actually that
Lord Dowding is right in saying that there should be more inspectors.

With regard to the inspection of animals producing veterinarv
sera, an. Order to control this was promised to us in Januarv 1949
and has not vet been brought in. Thisﬁbecause the Ministrv of
Agriculture is waiting until a committee which is sittina now has
decided on standards for the veterinarv products, such as those
required for sera for human use. I am told that as soon as the
committee has finished its work of standardizing these substances,
inspection will be introduced. F

fou ask whether I have visited haboratories where veterinarv ears
are produced. I have onlv seen the horses at the Lister Institute,
and I think You also saw them when you were there with us. Some of
these were uninspected horses. I am afraid I have not Vet visited
some of the small serum establishments which are not vet inspected:
I must try to do so. The animals involved would be almost entirelv
horses or, in the case of distemper serum, dogs. I

lou ask me also about hen batteries. ‘Ne discussed these at the
Summer School. Our German visitors are dead against them and against
their introduction into Germany, which apparentlv is iust beginning.
The English veterinary students were telling us that the dean-litter
system is becoming more and more popular, and that consider it far
more humane than the battery svstem; some of them even went so far
as to think that it will oust the batter? SVstem in time.

Unfortunatelv Dr. Lillie is not yet home from Canada, and so
was not able to speak at the Summer School.

We shall be very glad to see vou again, when You come over to
England. ,

--_/ .. J
Yours sincerelv, weéeL¢Q§Eéfgéuv “46

I now enclose 3 cepies of our 4th draft of law points
for overseas; this is not yet the finished article, but I hope it is a
bit clearer than the 5rd draft. It might be well for you to keep this
to your inner circle till we get out our final recommendations.

Now to your various points :-

1. Pain conditions. We have underlined the words "either""or" and "and“
because we thought perhaps it made things clearer. My suggestion was
to add at the end.of (ll) the words "even if the object of the
experiment has not been attained", but this was felt to be unnecessar
ii the other words were underlined.

Types of licence. You will see our suggestions in the new draft; this
section is not, however, in its final form.

Preference for dogs. I am very glad to have your views about this
and should like to talk this over with Hume when he comes back from
his holiday in France.

Debarking. No, I do not think it is generally doneth though it is
sometimes done, but I will find out for certain. Sound- prodf kennels

Schools. Our’8 Act does not apply to invertebrates. An experiment,
including a iceding experiment, on a vertebrate animal in a school
would be prosecutable,But this course has never been taken under
the 1876 Act. The animal is also protected by the Protection of
Animals Act 1911 and any cruelt to an animal in a school would
certainly be a contravention c? this Act. I will try to find out
Whether minor feeding eXperiments are ever done on vertebrate
animals in schools.
Ultimate responsibility. Here the ultimate responsibility for an
animal always rests on the licensee. I think No.20 in the new
draft is probably our final recommendation on this point.
Preparation of biological products, (a) medical, (b) veterinary.
‘Medical products come under the Therapeutic Substances Act and
the animals used are inspected by the‘Ministry of Health. Veter-
inary products come under the Diseases of Animals Act and, in spite*
of promises, animals are not yet inspected; they come under the
‘Ministry of Agriculture. In both cas imags used for testing.the
products are inspected under the 18765WE. am sending separately
copies of the Therapeutic Substances Act and the Diseases of Animals
Act.

U'F A W

284 Regent’s Rusk '
To President, Chairman & all
members of 8.8.0.

WORLD CONFERENCE OF MEDICAL EDUCATION.

Sectional meeting - "The laboratory in teaching eXperimental medicine"
Tuesday, August 25th, 1955.

Prof. L.P; Garrod who is to be the second sneaker has agreed to
.say that he considers that students using living animals ought to be
very strictly supervised.. This nay wive me a chance to join in the
subsequent discussion; if a) would you agree to my giving the
following as UFAW policy:-

(1) There should be strict supervision not only of the surgical
technique but also of the anaesthetic, and of the killing of the
animal.

12) We would much prefer all surgical work done by students to be done
on fully anaesthetised animals which are killed while under the
anaesthetic. We do not like recovery after surgical Operation by
students.

(3) We do not like repeatec major operations on the same animal. Our
Home Office practice is to deprecate the use of an animal for more than'
one major procedure.

(Not to be said — I have evidence that 9 Operations on one animal are
done by some American students.)

(4) If time allows I would mention British practice (see statement
below which the Home Office has passed as correct.)

Please send me your comments immediately; I shall take silence
as consent. ‘

Yours sincerely,

;2 t/CU! 641]?///

QFAW STATEMENT ON THE USE OF LIVING ANIMALS BY'STLW» ENTS IN E? ITAIN.

In Britain ODlY deeerebrate animals (in which the nasal gdnelia
have been destroyed) or pithed animals are used by undergracuate
students, exoept in the case of exceptional students among those
reading for an honours degree in physiology who must apply for a
licence from the Secretary of State in the usual way. A licence
is only granted to an undergraduate student if his work is so good
that his Professor recommends him to start on some original research.
Licences are usually only granted to students who will work under the
direct SD~l0€v‘3f“‘;7ision of a stated senior licence—holder.‘

No licences have been granted to undergraduates to use curare or
curariform drugs.

a medical student takes a 2-year course in physiology and
pharmacology and during this time sees,nbout 6 ~ 14 phySiolosy and
pharmacology demonstrations on anaesthetised ma ammals.‘ Demonstrations
are-carried out by a teacher who holds a special demonstration
certificate from the Secretary of State as well as a licence. The
animals must be killed at the end of the experiment. Demonstrations
are also done on decerebrate animals (in which the basal ganglia have
been destroyed). Demonstrations are frequently supplem nted by
films.

UGUST 1955.

President: Professor EDWARD HINDLE, M.A., Sc.D., PhD. F.R.S.

25th February 1955
Dear Mrs. Stevens,

We are at last able to send you our "explanatory leafﬂg‘et concerning the legal
control of laboratory animals in this country. I have sent: a few cepies also to
Captain Ioule, of Ottawa, Mr. Ricardo, of Vancouver, and Mr. Friedrichs of
San Francisco; and. to Dr. Robert Flynn, Argonne National Laboratory, Chicago. I am
not prOposinED to send copies to any other persons in the U.S.A. or Canada, as no
doubt you will do this if you think it advisable.

The following jourrnls helped, us o per the preparation of the UFAN Handbook on
the Care and Management of laboratory Aﬁﬂnals and might , I suppose, possiny review
this document {but you, of course, know much more about these than I do

Science JOUIml of the American Medical Association
Journal of the American Veterinary Medical Association
Canadian Medical Association Journal Canadian Journal of Comparative Medicine

I leave it to you entirely to do whatever you think best with these documents
in America, and we will gladly let you have further COpieS if they will be useful
to you.

I regret that your letter of Noverrber 17th is still unanswered. Here are a few
remarks concerning it:—

I don‘t think that at present any tally is kept of the numbers of animals used
in the preparation of veterinary pharmaceuticals, but I imagine the Ministry of
Health would. know the number of animals used for the preparation of human
phamaceuticals, though I am not at all sure .bout this. I am enclosing a copy of
the DiseaSes of Animals (Therapeutic Substances) Order, 1952, which we ‘have been
instrwnental in obtaining because of the lack of inspection of animals used for
veterirary pharmaceutical production. The most interesting paragraphs from. our
point of view are p.4 para. (1); page 11, Form 1; and page 14, l ( ). We have
had the assurance of the Ministry of Agriculture that inspection will include
inspection of premises overseas from which veterinary pharmaceuticals are imported
into this country - though I believe that very little importing is done at present.

We are having a stall at the forthcoming Animal Technicians Congress and
l

Eachibition, at which we are going to show ( sharpening of hypodermic needles by
means of a rotating grindstone, as used for blood transfusion work, and (2)
electrocution by means f a clip on the ear and a clip on the flank, as described
by Dr. Croft and Dr. Voute of Holland. In case you may by any chance be in this
country, the date is March 27th and 28th.

THE UNIVERSITIES FEDERATION FOR ANIMAL WELFARE

President: Professor EDWARD HINDLE, M.A., Sc.D., Ph.D. F.R.S.

Chairman -' KENNETH BIRD. C.B.E., B.Sc.. F.K.C.. A.M.I.C.E., F.S.I.A. (Fougasse) ,
Director: MAJOR c. w. HUME, M.C., B.Sc., R. Signals (Retd.) 284’ REGENT S PARK ROAD:

17th February, 1954.

Dear Mrs. Stevens,

I am sending you separately the Cat Film Strip and commentary,
price 5/6d, the Animal welfare Book List and three copies of the

Rabies Laws.

With regard to an address I gave on the animal protection
movement, it was, I think, based on the article which appeared in
the UFAW Courier No. 6, Spring 1952, and l am enclosing a copy.

The film "Traplines" and the mannequin parade went quite well,
though we did not have a very large audience.

I am much interested to hear that Dr. Hansen is going to see
the American Foudouk at Fez. It is a splendid piece of work and
far outstrips anything which SPATA has in North Africa. As a small
effort he might possibly be interested to see a little dog refuge
at Oran, in which case he should apply to Elle. Garious, 22 Avenne
Loubet, Oran. I imagine he would be staying at the Grand Hotel,
as it is much the best hotel in the town and not wildly expensive,
and Mlle. Garioux does not live very far from there. She would
try to be free to take him to the refuge which is on the outskirts

 




of the town.

At Casablanca, the Union marocaine pour la Protection des
Animaux has a refuge at 106 Rue du Riff in the ﬂouveau Hedina.
Me le Capitaine Aubron is always there in charge. SPAEA has
given financial help to this Society.

At Meknes there is a tiny tumbled-sown clinic bursting with
donkeys and a new one under construction. Dr. Hansen would need
to be escorted to these clinics and he conld contact Col. Eouyer
5 Rue Sidi Brahiﬁ, ﬁeknes.*» Col. Eonyer lives in the European
town. ' The Clinics are some way off in the ole town.

Rabat has been a disappointment so far. The refuge is only
half built and I am afraid we have nothing else in horocco. At
Algiers we have only so far a piece of land with a notice board on
it. Builéing licences are now coming through.

With kind regards,

Yours very sincerely,

THE UNIVERSITIES FEDERATION FOR ANIMAL WELFARE

President: Professor EDWARD HINDLE, M.A., Sc.D., Ph.D. F.R.S.

Chairman : KENNETH BIRD, C.B.E,, B,Sc.. F.K.C., A.M.I.C.E.. F.S.I.A. (Fougasse) 284 REGENTS PARK ROAD.

17th September 1955
Dear Mrs . Stevens,

First of all, thank you very much for letting me know about Mr.
John Macfarlane‘s report on the spaying of heifers, and thank you
very much for the information about rabies. I hOpe to study this
after the Summer School.

I am following your suggestions withregard to the American
Section of our Annual Report and I am omitting the reference to
controversy over dog—procurement, as this subject is beginning to
blow up here and UFAW wants to think things over before saying
anything in public.

With regard to the World Conference on Medical Education,
rrof. Garrod gave us a very nice opportunity in Section C. He said
that he considered that if animals were used by students they should
be carefully supervised and there shouII be no cruelty. I followed
up by mentioning;the points in the draft attached herewith, and Dr.
Lillie followed me with a rather diffuse'litule speech. My
reception was cold, though perhaps this was only to be expected.

The enclosed cutting, which I should be glad to have returned, is
quite inaccurate. The only thing I was aale to do was to get our
publishers to exhibit a few UFAW heafkats on their stand, and a few
copies of Hume ' 8 HOW to EsiﬂesLIsﬂessaiem islasls and EisLKiEisa
were taken. I think the Americans came over determined not to
mention the word "animal"; in speaking of experimental work they
never did mention it, and they were very guarded throughout. I

was dale to talk to a few of the overseas visitors, who were all
very pleasant and seemed to me to be high-principled.

Yours sincerely,

In Britain only decerebrate animals (in which the basal ganglia
have been destroyed) or pithed animals are used by undergraduate
students, xcept in the case of exceptional students among those
reading for an honours degree in physiology who must apply for a
licence from the Secretary of State in the usual way. A licence
is only granted to an undergraduate student if his J. ork is so mood
that his professor recommends him to start on some original resourcl.
Licences are usually only granted to students who will 1>rk under the
direct supervision of a stated senior licence holder. 5 t

No licences have been granted to undergraduates to use cur1nre or
curariform.drugs.

A medical student takes a 2— year course in physiology and

pharmacology and during this time sees about 6 14 physiology M d
pharmacology demonstrations on anaesthetised manuals. Demonstra ti ions
are 09 rried out by a teacher who holds a special denonstration
certificate from the Secretary of State as well as a licence. The
animals must be killed at the enc of the xperime:nt. Demonstrations
are also done on decerebrate animals (in which the basal ganglia have

been destroyed). Demonstrations are frequently suovleheated by

7th July 1955
Dear Mrs. Stevens,

I got‘hrss Bridgewood to ring me up, and she said she would write
to you at once, so I hOpe that by now you will have had a letter from
her. Apparently she never received your letters

I am finding out snout the present position of o‘egnency tests
here. The thing ehich I took exception to a little while ago was
that e Specimen of urine could be taken to e Chemist‘s shop and a
pregnancy test done upon it without any doctor coming into the picture.
That has now been stooped. The other point which I thought of taking
up was that there ere two methods of injecting the female Xenopus
for egg~leying tests-(fiBy Elken's method the injection is made
direct into the toedzs dorsal lymph sec.69ny the methoi now commonly
in use the needle is introduced under the skin of the thigh, is passed
up the leg, through the septum of the groin, and then up under the skin
of the back end so into the dorsal lymph sec, thisilonger route
being considered necessary to prevent leakage of the innoculum from
the See. This procedure epnaers to me to be of too greet.severity
to warrant its exclusion from the control of the Act, 'It is,
however, not controlled o; the 1876 Act“ WW. Gm Elken's advice
I did not proceed to do anything more about it. He evidently
feels that the procedure is not particularly severe.

I em eriting to or. Elken for informetion concerning uo—to—dete
methods of testing, and will let you know whet he says.

Yours sincerely,

Copy of letter from Dr. E. Elkan, Dept! of Pathology, Shrodells
Hospital, Willow Lane, Wetford.

8th July 1955
Dear Dr. Vinter,

Here in sstford we ere mainly using xenoous which hes remained
a very reliable test animal. The test is easy to perform and the
animals, when properly cared for, live 3 long time.

7::

ehen very quick results are required Bufo bufo (the male) is need.
This test is equally reliable but the husbandry of the animals is
not as simple as that of xenonus.

Very occasionally a COntrol is done on a rabbit (Friedman test)
but I do not think that this test has any advantage over the others,

We do not use mice (escheim-Zondek) but many hospitals still do.

In other countries the males of msny kinds of frogs have been
used for pregnancy tests, all, so far as I know, with quite e
good success.

When writing to the ﬁmericen Animal Welfare Institute, could you
ask them on my behalf if they can give me any edvice on how to
obtain live American selemenders, particularly plethodonts. I need

them.urgently and have made many attempts in various places with
little success. ,

Yours sincerely,

E. Elkan.

10th februery 1955
Dear Mrs. Stevens,

I am awfully sorry that all the clippings about the monkeys
seem to have been disposed of, excent for "he two which I encleee.
“he one from the New Stetemen was written after an interview that
Hume nd 33. had with tl’le Editor.

it was announced on the Wireless recently by an emericen medical
men tnet the monkeys which go to the National Foundation for Infantile
Paralysis are used for the production of a serum (or vaccine, l'em
notQLSure which was mentioned) after death, and that they were not
exoerimented on while alive. I have now been asked to find out
whéther this is neelly true. I imagine that the innoculeticn stage
is done during life, although the Serum (on vaccine} is perhaps
extracted after enaeethesie pnior to killing. If you could let me
know exectly whet the preceee is I should be grateful.

Yours einc

THE UNIVERSITIES FEDERATION FOR ANIMAL WELFARE

President: Professor EDWARD HINDLE, M.A.. Sc.D., Ph.D., F.R.S.

Chairman: KENNETH BIRD, C.B.E., B.Sc., F.K.C., A.M.I.C.E., F.S.I.A. (Fougasse) 7 L MB’
Director: MAJOR C. W. HUME, M.C., B.Sc., R. Signals (Retd.) A A S CONDUIT PASSAGE'
Technical Secretary: F. JEAN VINTER, M.D. LONDON. W.C.|.

Business Secretary : JOSEPHINE M. COZENS Telephone : CHAncery 922'

Branch Organizer : MARGARET P. WILLIAMS, 8.5:. (Econ.)
14th January 1955

Dear hrs. Stevens,

Journalists are a bit active here because of the death
from sufiocation at London Airport (heathrow) of some 500
monkeys in an unventilated van. This was quite an accident
should never recur, but it has brought to the fore the fact
that large numbers of monkeys are passing through London on
heir way from India to.America. is there anything that we
can say to journalists that would be of any help to you in your
work? I gather that some of the monkeys are used for rocket
research, as well as at the National Foundation for Infantile
Paralysis.

With regard to electrocution of degs at Delaware, could
you find out the following points: -

l. where are the electrodes applied?

2. What is the design of the electrodes?

5. What voltage is applied, and is it direct from the
mains and is it A.C. or 3.0.?

4. For how long is the current applied?

5. Do the animals develop a fit? This would be shown
by either rigidity or by paddling movements sitar
the currentmiswtugnedmgﬁﬁ? On the other hand is
the animal limp after switching off?

Has the experiment ever been made of passing the
current for one second, and then switching off? If
this is done, do the animals scream?

 

If we had the answers to these questions I could suggest
possible improvements.

The new edeetnne"Electrothanator' cabinet is only a
development of the Delaware method, the current being transformed
up to 500 V. and there being 5 electrodes instead of 2, to ensure
a transverse current through the brain as well as a longitudinal
current through the heart. Having transformed the current up
it is not completely safe to electrocute the dog outside a
cabinet. I did use 500 V. outside a cabinet in horocco, but
one feels a little anxious all the time that a human being may
get a shock. Perhaps the Delaware people could be frightened

p.t.o

 




into using a proPer cabinet by being told that they may
electroeute themselves without it. ' '

Thank you so much for the’copies of Comfortable
‘iua£E§£§~ It is a splendid publication, and i am glad
to have it for distribution.

 

I shall probably be abroad from the middle of Eebruary
to the middle of March.

with every good wish to you for health and happiness
and for your work in 1955. Shall we see you over here soon?

'Yours sincerely,

f“)
